2:31:2136:0:14:72:506:700:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:The Efficiency of Real-World Bargaining: Evidence from Wholesale Used-Auto Auctions
2:32:2219:14:14:72:146:686:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Bradley Larsen
2:33:2233:14:14:72:234:672:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:NBER Working Paper No. 20431
2:34:2261:14:14:72:134:658:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:August 2014
2:35:2272:14:28:72:245:644:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:JEL No. C57,C78,D44,D47,D82,L1
2:36:2302:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:C:ABSTRACT
2:37:2310:28:14:72:540:588:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:This study quantifies the efficiency of a real-world bargaining game with two-sided incomplete information.
2:38:2417:14:14:72:540:574:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) and Williams (1987) derived the theoretical efficient frontier for
2:39:2516:14:14:72:540:560:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:bilateral trade under two-sided uncertainty, but little is known about how well real-world bargaining
2:40:2617:14:14:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:performs relative to the frontier. The setting is wholesale used-auto auctions, an $80 billion industry
2:41:2720:14:14:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:where buyers and sellers participate in alternating-offer bargaining when the auction price fails to
2:42:2820:14:14:72:540:518:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:reach a secret reserve price. Using 270,000 auction/bargaining sequences, this study nonparametrically
2:43:2922:14:14:72:540:504:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:estimates bounds on the distributions of buyer and seller valuations and then estimates where bargaining
2:44:3026:14:14:72:540:490:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:outcomes lie relative to the efficient frontier. Findings indicate that the dynamic mechanism attains
2:45:3127:14:42:72:408:476:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:80-91% of the surplus which can be achieved on the efficient frontier.
2:46:3197:42:14:72:146:434:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Bradley Larsen
2:47:3211:14:14:72:198:420:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Department of Economics
2:48:3234:14:14:72:167:406:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Stanford University
2:49:3253:14:14:72:144:392:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:579 Serra Mall
2:50:3267:14:14:72:169:378:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Stanford, CA 94305
2:51:3285:14:14:72:124:364:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:and NBER
2:52:3293:14:0:72:181:350:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:bjlarsen@stanford.edu
3:53:3314:0:25:72:185:681:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:1    Introduction
3:54:3331:25:16:72:526:656:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:From haggling in an open-street market to negotiating a corporate takeover deal, alternating-offer bar-
3:55:3434:16:15:72:526:640:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:gaining between a buyer and seller is one of the oldest and most common forms of transaction.  When both
3:56:3538:15:16:72:525:625:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:parties have incomplete information, it is known that equilibrium outcomes are difficult to characterize.1
3:57:3644:16:15:72:526:609:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) demonstrated that full efficiency is not possible, and theoretical effi-
3:58:3749:15:16:72:526:594:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:ciency bounds are derived in Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) and Williams (1987), but it is unknown
3:59:3849:16:15:72:526:578:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:how well real-world bargaining performs relative to these bounds.  Williams (1987) emphasized that “little
3:60:3955:15:16:72:526:563:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is known about whether or not these limits can be achieved with ‘realistic’ bargaining procedures.”  This
3:61:4060:16:16:72:526:547:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:paper is the first attempt to bring data to this question in order to quantify the efficiency of real-world
3:62:4167:16:15:72:526:531:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:bargaining with two-sided incomplete information.  I develop a framework to estimate distributions of
3:63:4268:15:16:72:526:516:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private valuations on both sides of the market at wholesale used-auto auctions.  I then map these prim-
3:64:4371:16:15:72:526:500:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:itives into results from the theoretical mechanism design literature in order to measure the efficiency of
3:65:4477:15:16:72:526:485:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:bargaining relative to the first-best frontier and the information-constrained, second-best efficient frontier.
3:66:4588:16:15:86:525:469:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The  data  analyzed  in  this  paper  consist  of  several  hundred  thousand  sequences  of  back-and-forth
3:67:4696:15:16:72:526:454:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:bargaining offers between buyers and sellers at wholesale used-auto auctions, a large market where new
3:68:4798:16:15:72:526:438:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and used-car dealers buy vehicles from other dealers as well as from rental companies and banks.  This
3:69:4900:15:16:72:526:423:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:industry  passes  15  million  cars  annually  through  its  lanes,  selling  about  60%  of  the  vehicles,  worth  a
3:70:5018:16:15:72:525:407:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:total of$80 billion.  Throughout much of the industry, auction houses employ the following mechanism:
3:71:5119:15:16:72:526:392:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:a secret reserve price set by the seller followed by an ascending price auction,  which,  when the secret
3:72:5224:16:16:72:526:376:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:reserve price is not met, is followed by post-auction, alternating-offer bargaining mediated by the auction
3:73:5331:16:15:72:526:360:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:house.  This setting is ideal for studying bargaining under two-sided uncertainty because all players are
3:74:5436:15:16:72:526:345:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:experienced professionals and are likely to understand well the game being played.  Also,  because the
3:75:5538:16:15:72:526:329:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:bargaining takes place after an ascending auction and after the seller’s choice of a secret reserve price,
3:76:5644:15:16:72:526:314:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the efficiency of bargaining can be studied while imposing only minimal assumptions on the structure or
3:77:5747:16:15:72:228:298:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:equilibrium of the bargaining game.
3:78:5782:15:16:86:525:283:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:After  a  brief  introduction  to  the  industry,  Section  2  discusses  the  data  in  detail.   The  data  comes
3:79:5897:16:15:72:526:267:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:from several different auction houses from 2007 to 2010, containing detailed information on each car as
3:80:6000:15:16:72:526:252:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:well as the actions taken by sellers and buyers in each stage of the game.  The data is broken down into
3:81:6104:16:15:72:526:236:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:two main samples:  cars sold by used and new-car dealers (which I refer to as the dealers sample), and
3:82:6206:15:16:72:526:221:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:cars sold by large institutions, such as rental companies, banks, and fleet companies (which I refer to as
3:83:6312:16:15:72:526:205:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the fleet/lease sample).  Fleet/lease sellers tend to set reserve prices which are not as high above auction
3:84:6420:15:16:72:485:190:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:prices as are the reserve prices set by dealers, and hence fleet/lease cars are more likely to sell.
3:85:6520:16:15:86:525:174:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:I lay out a simple model in Section 3 which describes the three stages of the game at wholesale auto
3:86:6620:15:15:83:525:159:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:1Fudenberg  and  Tirole  (1991)  stated,  “The  theory  of  bargaining  under  incomplete  information  is  currently  more  a
3:87:6746:15:12:72:526:144:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:series of examples than a coherent set of results.  This is unfortunate because bargaining derives much of its interest from
3:88:6870:12:12:72:526:132:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:incomplete information.”  Fudenberg, Levine, and Tirole (1985) similarly commented “We fear that in this case [of two-sided
3:89:6993:12:13:72:526:120:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:incomplete information], few generalizations will be possible, and that even for convenient specifications of the functional
3:90:7117:13:0:72:462:107:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:form of the distribution of valuations, the problem of characterizing the equilibria will be quite difficult.”
4:91:7228:0:15:72:526:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auctions.  The post-auction bargaining is modeled as a general alternating-offer bargaining game.  The
4:92:7330:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auction  stage  is  modeled  as  an  ascending  auction  with  private  values  among  bidders,  where  bidders’
4:93:7442:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:values (as well as the seller’s value) are correlated through auction-level heterogeneity unobserved by the
4:94:7549:15:16:72:526:635:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:econometrician.  The seller’s secret reserve price is chosen optimally before the auction.  I prove three
4:95:7654:16:16:72:526:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:preliminary results which motivate an estimation strategy:  first, truth-telling is a dominant strategy for
4:96:7761:16:15:72:526:603:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:bidders in the auction; second, the seller’s secret reserve price strategy is monotone; and third, auction-
4:97:7868:15:16:72:526:588:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:level heterogeneity affects the outcomes of the game in a tractable manner.  These three properties allow
4:98:7973:16:15:72:526:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for nonparametric identification of the distributions of buyer and seller types and the region of the type
4:99:8079:15:16:72:195:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:space in which trade occurs.
4:100:8107:16:15:86:525:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Sections 4 and 5 constitute the heart of the paper.  Section 4 presents the approach for estimating the
4:101:8210:15:16:72:526:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:distributions of buyer and seller valuations.  After controlling for observable covariates and auction house
4:102:8318:16:15:72:526:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:fees, I account for unobserved heterogeneity at the auction level, relying on a deconvolution argument due
4:103:8424:15:16:72:526:495:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to Kotlarski (1967) for identification.  I then estimate the distribution of buyer valuations using an order
4:104:8532:16:15:72:526:479:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:statistic inversion.  The approach for estimating the distribution of seller types is new.  It exploits bounds
4:105:8642:15:16:72:526:464:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:defined  by  revealed  preferences  arguments  taken  from  the  seller’s  response  to  the  auction  price.   The
4:106:8757:16:15:72:526:448:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:approach is similar in spirit to Haile and Tamer (2003), using bounds implied by very basic assumptions
4:107:8860:15:16:72:526:433:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:about  players’  rationality  to  learn  about  model  primitives  without  imposing  a  complete  model  of  the
4:108:8973:16:16:72:225:417:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:game or solving for an equilibrium.
4:109:9008:16:15:86:525:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Section 5 presents the methods for estimating the efficient frontier and other counterfactual mecha-
4:110:9108:15:16:72:526:386:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:nisms from mechanism design theory.  Throughout the paper, the terms “efficient” or “second-best” refer
4:111:9211:16:15:72:526:370:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to ex-ante incentive efficiency, taking informational asymmetries into account.  To refer to full efficiency,
4:112:9320:15:16:72:525:355:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:I use the terms “ex-post efficient” or “first-best.”2I also use the terms “surplus” and “gains from trade”
4:113:9426:16:15:72:526:339:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:interchangeably.  The efficient frontier (or Pareto frontier) delineates the best possible outcomes, in terms
4:114:9535:15:16:72:526:324:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of buyer and seller surplus, that could be achieved by any bilateral bargaining game in the presence of
4:115:9638:16:15:72:526:308:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:two-sided incomplete information.  Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) and Williams (1987) demonstrated
4:116:9738:15:16:72:526:293:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:how this frontier depends on the distributions of buyer and seller valuations.  Therefore, the estimated
4:117:9842:16:15:72:526:277:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:distributions from Section 4 are crucial in solving for these mechanisms.  To solve for the Pareto frontier,
4:118:9950:15:16:72:526:262:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:I adopt an approach described in Williams (1987).  I also demonstrate that the direct-revelation mech-
4:119:10052:16:16:72:526:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:anism corresponding to the dynamic bargaining mechanism currently used at auto auctions is identified
4:120:10153:16:15:72:526:230:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:from information on whether trade occurred and from secret reserve and auction prices, relying on the
4:121:10254:15:16:72:384:215:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:property that the seller’s secret reserve price will be strictly monotonic.
4:122:10329:16:15:86:525:199:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:I then combine the estimated distributions of buyer and seller valuations and the allocation functions
4:123:10431:15:16:72:526:184:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:defining the current mechanism and counterfactual mechanisms, putting theoretical bargaining and real-
4:124:10533:16:15:72:526:168:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:world  bargaining  on  the  same  footing  in  order  to  quantify  bargaining  efficiency.   First,  I  examine  the
4:125:10650:15:16:72:526:153:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:efficiency loss due to incomplete information.  Ideally, a buyer and seller should trade whenever the buyer
4:126:10757:16:15:72:526:137:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:values a good more than the seller (first-best, ex-post efficient trade).  However, incomplete information
4:127:10863:15:15:83:525:122:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:2For a more detailed taxonomy of ex-ante, interim, and ex-post efficiency under incomplete information, see Holmstr ̈om
4:128:10982:15:0:72:148:107:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:and Myerson (1983).
5:129:11002:0:15:72:526:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:on  both  sides  gives  rise  to  a  bilateral  monopoly,  where  each  party  has  some  market  power.   Myerson
5:130:11116:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and Satterthwaite (1983) demonstrated that a deadweight loss occurs as each party trades off the dual
5:131:11217:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:incentives of increasing the probability of trade and extracting additional rent from the other party, akin
5:132:11324:15:16:72:526:635:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to the deadweight loss in a standard one-sided monopoly pricing model.  As a result, some trades fail to
5:133:11428:16:16:72:525:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:occur even when the buyer values the good more than the seller.3This deadweight loss is given by the
5:134:11528:16:15:71:525:603:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:gap between the the second-best mechanism derived in Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) and first-best
5:135:11628:15:16:71:525:588:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:trade.  I discover that incomplete information need not be a huge problem in this market: The second-best
5:136:11733:16:15:71:519:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:mechanism achieves about 99% of first-best surplus and over 90% of the first-best probability of trade.
5:137:11836:15:16:86:525:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Second,  I  examine  the  efficiency  of  post-auction  bargaining  relative  to  the  information-constrained
5:138:11946:16:15:71:525:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:efficient frontier.  Unlike the mechanisms discussed in Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) and Williams
5:139:12047:15:16:71:525:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(1987), alternating-offer bargaining with two-sided uncertainty has no clear equilibrium predictions due
5:140:12151:16:15:71:525:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to signaling by both parties.  As a result,  it is unknown where alternating-offer bargaining lies within
5:141:12256:15:16:71:525:495:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the  efficient  frontier.   Any  gap  between  the  efficient  frontier  and  real-world  bargaining  represents  a
5:142:12371:16:15:71:525:479:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:deadweight loss which could theoretically be eliminated by committing to a static efficient mechanism
5:143:12472:15:16:71:525:464:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:along  the  frontier.   Therefore,  I  refer  to  this  as  the  deadweight  loss  due  to  mechanism  choice/limited
5:144:12589:16:15:71:525:448:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:commitment.4Findings indicate that the post-auction bargaining achieves 80–84% of the efficient level
5:145:12690:15:16:71:525:433:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of surplus in the dealers sample and 87–91% in fleet/lease sample.  The deadweight loss due to limited
5:146:12792:16:16:71:313:417:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:commitment is therefore roughly 9–20% in this market.
5:147:12845:16:15:86:525:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In addition to Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) and Williams (1987), theoretical papers examining
5:148:12942:15:16:71:525:386:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the efficient frontier from a mechanism design standpoint include Ausubel and Deneckere (1993), Ausubel,
5:149:13046:16:15:71:525:370:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Cramton,  and  Deneckere  (2002),  Satterthwaite  and  Williams  (1989),  and  Chatterjee  and  Samuelson
5:150:13151:15:16:71:525:355:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(1983).   Ausubel  and  Deneckere  (1993)  and  Ausubel,  Cramton,  and  Deneckere  (2002)  demonstrated
5:151:13255:16:15:71:525:339:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:theoretically that when buyer and seller distributions have monotone hazard rates and when high weights
5:152:13358:15:16:71:525:324:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are placed on the seller or buyer payoff, some equilibria of a dynamic, alternating-offer bargaining game
5:153:13463:16:15:71:525:308:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:can reach the efficient frontier.  Satterthwaite and Williams (1989) studied thekdouble auction game and
5:154:13567:15:16:71:525:293:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:found that generically only thek= 0 ork= 1 double auctions reach the efficient frontier.5Chatterjee
5:155:13666:16:15:71:525:277:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and  Samuelson  (1983)  demonstrated  that  in  the  symmetric  uniform  case  thek=  1/2  double  auction
5:156:13772:15:16:71:525:262:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:also reaches the efficient frontier.  To my knowledge, the current paper is the first to bring data to the
5:157:13878:16:16:71:368:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:framework of Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) or Williams (1987).
5:158:13943:16:15:86:525:230:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:A  large  theoretical  literature  on  incomplete-information  strategic  bargaining  has  yielded  valuable
5:159:14051:15:15:71:525:215:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:insights through focusing on special cases rather than the full, two-sided incomplete information setting.6
5:160:14158:15:15:83:525:200:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:3Formally, Myerson and Satterthwaite (1983) demonstrated that when the supports of buyer and seller types overlap,
5:161:14272:15:12:72:526:185:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:there  does  not  exist  an  incentive-compatible,  individually  rational  mechanism  which  is  ex-post  efficient  and  which  also
5:162:14406:12:8:72:173:173:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:satisfies a balanced budget.
5:163:14434:8:10:83:508:165:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:4Cramton (1992) and Elyakime, Laffont, Loisel, and Vuong (1997) also referred to this as an issue of commitment.
5:164:14546:10:15:83:525:155:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:5Akdouble auction consists of both the buyer and seller simultaneously reporting sealed bids to an intermediary and,
5:165:14662:15:8:72:509:140:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:if the buyer’s bid exceeds the seller’s, trading at a price which is a convex combination of the two bids, with weightk.
5:166:14782:8:15:83:525:132:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:6The incomplete-information bargaining literature focuses on settings of one-sided uncertainty (Gul, Sonnenschein, and
5:167:14900:15:12:71:525:117:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Wilson 1986; Gul and Sonnenschein 1988; Fudenberg and Tirole 1983; Sobel and Takahashi 1983; Fudenberg, Levine, and
5:168:15015:12:0:71:525:105:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Tirole 1985; Ausubel and Deneckere 1989; Rubinstein 1985a,b; Bikhchandani 1992; Grossman and Perry 1986; Admati and
6:169:15131:0:15:72:526:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The set of empirical papers which structurally estimated models of incomplete information bargaining
6:170:15231:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is  quite  small,  including  Sieg  (2000),  Ambrus,  Chaney,  and  Salitsky  (2011),  and  Silveira  (2012),  who
6:171:15345:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:focused on settings of one-sided incomplete-information bargaining, and Keniston (2011), who estimated
6:172:15447:15:16:72:525:635:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:a model of two-sided uncertainty and compared alternating-offer bargaining to a fixed-price mechanism.7
6:173:15550:16:16:72:526:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:One advantage of the current paper over previous structural papers is that, because the bargaining occurs
6:174:15655:16:15:72:526:603:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:after an auction and after the seller reports a secret reserve price,  the model’s primitives,  namely the
6:175:15761:15:16:72:526:588:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:distributions of buyer and seller valuations, can be identified from these pre-bargaining actions without
6:176:15866:16:15:72:526:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:relying on much structure or on a complete model of the bargaining game.  This is particularly useful
6:177:15967:15:16:72:526:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:given that, unlike auction settings or complete-information bargaining games, there is no canonical model
6:178:16072:16:38:72:460:541:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:of alternating-offer bargaining under incomplete information with a continuum of types.8
6:179:16160:38:25:72:369:503:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:2    The Wholesale Auto Auction Industry
6:180:16200:25:16:72:526:478:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The wholesale used-auto auction industry provides liquidity to the supply side of the US used-car market.
6:181:16305:16:16:72:526:462:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Each  year  approximately  40  million  used  cars  are  sold  in  the  United  States,  15  million  of  which  pass
6:182:16422:16:15:72:525:446:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:through a wholesale auction house.  About 60% of these cars sell, with an average price between$8,000
6:183:16523:15:16:72:525:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and$9,000, totaling to over$80 billion in revenue (NAAA 2009).  The industry consists of approximately
6:184:16625:16:15:72:526:415:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:320 auction houses scattered across the country.  The industry leaders, Manheim and Adesa, maintain
6:185:16724:15:16:72:526:400:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:about a 50% and 25% market share, respectively, and the remaining auction houses are referred to as
6:186:16823:16:15:72:526:384:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:independents.  Each auction house serves as a platform in a two-sided market, competing to attract both
6:187:16926:15:16:72:526:369:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:sellers  and  buyers.   Throughout  the  industry,  the  majority  of  auction  house  revenue  comes  from  fees
6:188:17039:16:17:72:526:353:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:paid by the buyer and seller when trade occurs.  Buyers attending wholesale auto auctions are new and
6:189:17140:17:13:72:526:336:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Perry 1987; Cramton 1991), settings of one-sided offers (Cho 1990; Feinberg and Skrzypacz 2005; Cramton 1984; Ausubel
6:190:17257:13:12:72:526:323:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:and Deneckere 1993), settings with two-types rather than a continuum of types (Chatterjee and Samuelson 1988; Compte
6:191:17373:12:12:72:526:311:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:and Jehiel 2002), or settings with uncertainty not being about valuations (Abreu and Gul 2000; Watson 1998).  Two papers
6:192:17493:12:12:72:526:299:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:which modeled bargaining as an alternating-offer game and a continuum of types with two-sided incomplete information,
6:193:17610:12:13:72:526:287:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:where the incomplete information is about players’ valuations, are Perry (1986), which predicted immediate agreement or
6:194:17729:13:12:72:526:274:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:disagreement, and Cramton (1992), which modeled the bargaining game as beginning with a war of attrition and consisting
6:195:17848:12:12:72:526:262:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:of players signaling their valuations through the length of delay between offers, as in Admati and Perry (1987).  Neither
6:196:17969:12:13:72:526:250:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:of these models fits the type of bargaining observed at wholesale auto auctions.  See Binmore,  Osborne,  and Rubinstein
6:197:18089:13:12:72:526:237:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:(1992), Ausubel, Cramton, and Deneckere (2002), Roth (1995), and Kennan and Wilson (1993) for additional surveys of
6:198:18204:12:8:72:274:225:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:the theoretical and experimental bargaining literature.
6:199:18259:8:15:83:525:217:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:7A separate strand of literature,  such as Tang and Merlo (2010),  Watanabe (2009),  Crawford and Yurukoglu (2012),
6:200:18374:15:12:72:525:202:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Grennan (2013), and others, estimated structural models which included elements of bargaining undercompleteinformation
6:201:18492:12:8:72:205:190:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:rather than incomplete information.
6:202:18527:8:15:83:525:182:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:8Several papers analyzed models of auctions followed by complete-information bargaining (Huh and Park 2010; Elyakime,
6:203:18644:15:12:72:526:167:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Laffont, Loisel, and Vuong 1997), one-shot bargaining (Bulow and Klemperer 1996; Eklof and Lunander 2003; Menezes and
6:204:18761:12:12:72:526:155:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Ryan  2005),  or  one-sided  incomplete  information  (Wang  2000).   Genesove  (1991)  also  discussed  post-auction  bargaining
6:205:18890:12:13:72:526:143:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:at wholesale auto auctions.  He tested several parametric assumptions for the distributions of buyer and seller valuations,
6:206:19013:13:0:72:506:130:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:finding that these assumptions performed poorly in explaining when bargaining occurred and when it was successful.
7:207:19128:0:15:72:525:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:used car dealers.9Sellers may be used or new car dealers (whom I will refer to as “dealers”) selling off
7:208:19232:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:extra inventory,  or they may be large institutions,  such as banks,  manufacturers,  or rental companies
7:209:19337:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(whom I will refer to as “fleet/lease”) selling repossessed, off-lease, lease-buy-back, or old fleet vehicles.
7:210:19447:15:16:72:372:635:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Throughout, I will refer to the seller as “she” and the buyer as “he”.
7:211:19517:16:16:86:525:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Sellers bring their cars to the auction house and establish a secret reserve price.10In the days preceding
7:212:19623:16:15:72:526:603:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the auctioning of the car, potential buyers may view car details and pictures online, including a condition
7:213:19730:15:16:72:525:588:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:report for cars sold by fleet/lease sellers, or may visit the auction house to inspect and test drive cars.11
7:214:19839:16:15:72:526:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The auction sale takes place in a large, warehouse-like room with 8–16 lanes running through it.  In each
7:215:19944:15:16:72:526:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:lane there is a separate auctioneer, and lanes run simultaneously.  A car is driven to the front of the lane
7:216:20052:16:15:72:526:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and the auctioneer calls out bids, raising the price until only one bidder remains.  The characteristics of
7:217:20159:15:16:72:526:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the car as well as the current bid are listed on a large monitor near the auctioneer.  The entire bidding
7:218:20264:16:15:72:230:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:process usually takes 30–90 seconds.
7:219:20300:15:16:86:525:495:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:If the auction price exceeds the secret reserve price,  the car is awarded to the high bidder.  If the
7:220:20402:16:15:72:526:479:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auction  price  is  below  the  secret  reserve,  the  high  bidder  is  given  the  option  to  enter  into  bargaining
7:221:20522:15:16:72:526:464:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with the seller.  If the high bidder opts to bargain, the auction house will contact the seller by phone,
7:222:20627:16:15:72:525:448:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:at which point the seller can accept the auction price, end the negotiations, or propose a counteroffer.12
7:223:20733:15:16:72:526:433:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:If the seller counters,  the auction house calls the buyer.  Bargaining continues until one party accepts
7:224:20838:16:16:72:525:417:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:or  terminates  negotiations.   The  typical  time  between  calls  is  2–3  hours.13Auction  house  employees
7:225:20948:16:15:72:526:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:contacting each party take care not to reveal the other party’s identity in order to prevent the buyer and
7:226:21054:15:15:72:466:386:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:seller from agreeing on a trade outside of the auction house, avoiding auction house fees.14
7:227:21146:15:15:83:525:371:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:9Note that the term “new” means the dealer is authorized to sell new cars from a manufacturer, but can also sell used
7:228:21263:15:12:72:526:356:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:cars.  On the other hand, “used” car dealers can only sell used cars.  Genesove (1993) discussed the differences of cars sold
7:229:21388:12:13:72:526:344:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:by new vs.  used-car dealers and found weak evidence of adverse selection among cars sold by used-car dealers.  Note also
7:230:21509:13:12:72:526:331:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:that the general public is not allowed at these auctions; Hammond and Morrill (2014) presented a model explaining this
7:231:21627:12:7:72:162:319:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:feature of auto auctions.
7:232:21652:7:16:79:525:312:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:10Most sellers report this secret reserve price to the auction house.  Some sellers choose not to report their reserve as they
7:233:21778:16:12:72:526:296:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:plan to either be present during the auction or to await a phone call from the auction house informing them of the auction
7:234:21900:12:7:72:226:284:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:price prior to deciding whether to accept.
7:235:21942:7:16:79:525:277:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:11According to conversations with participants and personal observations, few buyers appear to visit the auction house
7:236:22060:16:7:72:159:261:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:prior to the day of sale.
7:237:22085:7:15:79:525:254:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:12If  the  seller  a  present  during  the  auctioning  of  the  car,  the  seller  may  choose  to  accept  or  reject  the  auction  price
7:238:22225:15:12:72:526:239:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:immediately.  If the seller is not present but the auctioneer observes that the auction price and the reserve price are far
7:239:22348:12:13:72:526:227:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:enough apart that phone bargaining is very unlikely to succeed, the auctioneer may choose to immediately reject the auction
7:240:22471:13:7:72:175:214:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:price on behalf of the seller.
7:241:22501:7:15:79:525:207:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:13During the time a car is in the bargaining process, or if that bargaining has ended in no trade, interested buyers other
7:242:22623:15:13:72:526:192:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:than the high bidder may also contact the auction house and place offers on the car.  If the bargaining between the original
7:243:22747:13:12:72:526:179:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:high bidder and seller ends in disagreement, bargaining commences with the next interested buyer as long as his offer is
7:244:22867:12:12:72:526:167:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:higher than previous offers the seller has rejected.  This occurs for about three percent of the cars in the full dataset.  This
7:245:22995:12:13:72:526:155:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:separate form of dynamics is not accounted for in the model below the observations where this occurs are not included in
7:246:23115:13:7:72:118:142:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:the analysis.
7:247:23128:7:15:79:525:135:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:14The identity of a fleet/lease seller is revealed, and these sellers tend to try to build a positive reputation of not setting
7:248:23255:15:13:72:526:120:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:excessively high reserve prices.  Conversations with industry participants reveal that at some auction houses outside of the
7:249:23379:13:0:72:362:107:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:data sample studied in this paper, the identity of small sellers is also revealed.
8:250:23462:0:15:86:525:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:A seller accepting the auction price (or bargaining offers) below the reserve price may seem puzzling
8:251:23563:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:given that the seller could have accomplished an equivalent outcome by reporting a lower secret reserve
8:252:23666:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:price.  Industry participants explain this phenomenon as having several potential causes:  sellers system-
8:253:23772:15:16:72:526:635:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:ically  set  high  reserve  prices  due  to  overly  optimistic  beliefs  about  auction  prices  (Treece  2013)  or  in
8:254:23892:16:16:72:526:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:attempt to influence auctioneers to achieve higher prices (Lacetera, Larsen, Pope, and Sydnor 2014), or
8:255:23995:16:15:72:398:603:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:sellers are learning something about demand after observing the auction.15
8:256:24069:15:16:86:525:588:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:If the auction and/or bargaining does not result in trade the first time the vehicle is up for sale (or
8:257:24172:16:15:71:525:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:first “run”), the vehicle can either be taken back to the seller’s business location or, more often, remain
8:258:24279:15:16:71:525:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:at the auction house until the next available sales opportunity, usually several weeks later.16The seller
8:259:24384:16:15:71:525:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:can change her reserve price before the next run of the vehicle.  If trade takes place but the buyer feels he
8:260:24493:15:16:71:525:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:has been given a lemon, he may, under certain conditions, request arbitration, in which the auction house
8:261:24598:16:15:71:525:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:intervenes to either undo the sale or negotiate a lower sale price.  This occurs less than three percent of
8:262:24705:15:16:71:160:495:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the time in practice.
8:263:24726:16:15:86:525:479:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The  dataset  used  in  this  paper  is  new  to  the  literature.   The  data  come  from  six  auction  houses,
8:264:24839:15:16:71:525:464:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:each maintaining a large market share in the region in which it operates.  Between January 2007 and
8:265:24938:16:15:71:525:448:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:March 2010 these auction houses passed over 600,000 vehicles through their lanes.  The data from these
8:266:25040:15:16:71:525:433:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auction houses includes detailed information on each car, including make, model, year, trim, and odometer
8:267:25145:16:16:71:525:417:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:reading; condition report (prepared by the auction house for fleet/lease vehicles); a blue book estimate
8:268:25249:16:15:71:525:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:provided by the auction house; the number of pictures displayed in the online pre-sale profile of the car;
8:269:25355:15:16:71:499:386:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:disclosure codes for different types of damage reported by the seller; and the identity of the seller.
8:270:25457:16:15:86:525:370:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:An observation in the dataset represents a run of the vehicle, that is, a distinct attempt to sell the
8:271:25559:15:16:71:525:355:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:vehicle  through  the  auction  or,  if  the  reserve  price  is  not  met,  through  post-auction  bargaining.   The
8:272:25676:16:15:71:525:339:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:total  number  of  runs  recorded  in  the  data  is  approximately  1,000,000,  so  on  average  a  vehicle  passes
8:273:25792:15:16:71:525:324:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:through the lanes 1.67 times.  I treat each run as an independent observation and do not model dynamics
8:274:25895:16:15:71:525:308:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:between  runs.   For  a  given  run,  the  data  records  the  date,  time,  auction  house  location,  and  auction
8:275:26011:15:16:71:525:293:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:lane, as well as the seller’s secret reserve price, the auction price, and, when bargaining occurs over the
8:276:26118:16:15:71:525:277:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:phone, the full sequence of buyer and seller actions (accept, quit, or counter), and the amounts of any
8:277:26221:15:16:71:157:262:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:offers/counteroffers.
8:278:26242:16:16:86:525:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:I drop observations with no recorded auction house blue book estimate;  cars less than one year or
8:279:26340:16:15:71:525:230:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:greater than 16 years old; cars with less than 100 miles or greater than 300,000 miles on the odometer;
8:280:26443:15:16:71:525:215:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:observations in which the auction sale timestamp is missing;  and observations for which the following
8:281:26545:16:15:71:525:199:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:variables lie outside their respective 0.01 and 0.99 quantiles:  auction price, reserve price, blue book price,
8:282:26656:15:16:71:525:184:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:or the gap between the reserve and auction price.  I drop observations for which fewer than ten vehicles
8:283:26760:16:14:71:525:168:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:were observed at a given make-model-year-trim-age combination or days in which fewer than 100 cars
8:284:26858:14:16:79:525:154:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:15For the estimation approach I adopt, knowing the precise data-generating process for reserve prices is unnecessary; it
8:285:26978:16:12:72:526:138:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:is only necessary that secret reserve prices be strictly increasing in the seller’s underlying true valuation, a result generated
8:286:27107:12:7:72:170:126:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:by the model in Section 3.
8:287:27133:7:16:79:525:119:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:16Genesove (1995) presented a search model to study the seller’s decision to reject the auction price and take the car back
8:288:27256:16:0:72:141:103:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:to her own car lot.
9:289:27276:0:43:233:363:680:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 1:  Descriptive statistics
9:290:27308:43:16:223:459:637:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:means.d.means.d.
9:291:27324:16:15:116:464:621:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Trade0.7100.4540.7600.427
9:292:27349:15:16:116:466:606:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Reserve price$7,379$5,196$10,307$5,764
9:293:27387:16:16:116:466:590:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Auction price$6,256$4,923$9,826$5,846
9:294:27424:16:15:116:466:574:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Blue book$6,811$4,853$10,964$6,140
9:295:27458:15:16:116:464:559:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Age (years)6.7813.3803.1502.558
9:296:27489:16:31:116:466:543:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Mileage97,94146,51857,17640,318
9:297:27520:31:0:116:442:512:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Sample size135,942136,453
9:298:27545:0:169:231:463:654:9:g_d0_f10:9:g_d0_f10:F:Dealers sampleFleet/lease sample
9:299:27577:169:14:72:522:485:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:F:Notes:  Trade is an indicator for whether trade occurred between the buyer and seller.  Blue book is an estimate
9:300:27689:14:32:72:322:471:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:F:of the market value of the car, provided by the auction house.
9:301:27751:32:16:72:526:439:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:were offered for sale at a given auction house.  I drop observations where the auction price or reserve price
9:302:27860:16:15:72:525:423:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is missing or is equal to zero and incomplete bargaining sequences.17In the end, I am left with 135,942
9:303:27963:15:16:72:526:408:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:runs of cars offered for sale by used-car dealers (which I will refer to as the dealers sample), and 136,453
9:304:28071:16:15:72:436:392:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:offered for sale by fleet/lease sellers (which I will refer to as the fleet/lease sample).
9:305:28161:15:16:86:525:377:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Summary statistics are displayed in Table 1.  The probability of trade is 0.71 in the dealers sample
9:306:28261:16:15:72:526:361:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and  slightly  higher  in  the  fleet/lease  sample.   In  the  dealers  sample,  the  average  auction  price  is  over
9:307:28381:15:16:72:525:346:10:g_d0_f385R:10:g_d0_f3:B:$1,000 below the average reserve price and about$600 below the average blue book price.  Dealer cars are
9:308:28485:16:15:72:526:330:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:on average seven years old and have nearly 100,000 miles on the odometer.  Fleet/lease cars tend to be
9:309:28587:15:16:72:526:315:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:newer (three years old and 57,000 miles) and higher priced and have a smaller gap between the reserve
9:310:28688:16:15:72:526:299:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and auction prices.  Also, unlike dealer cars, in fleet/lease cars the reserve price does not exceed the blue
9:311:28797:15:16:72:526:284:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:book price on average.  All of these descriptive statistics are consistent with conversations with industry
9:312:28904:16:16:72:526:268:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:participants:  Dealer cars tend to be older cars with more aggressive reserve prices and be less likely to
9:313:29010:16:15:72:89:252:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:sell.
9:314:29015:15:15:86:525:237:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 2 displays the characteristics of observations in the dealers sample separated by the period of
9:315:29116:15:15:79:525:222:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:17Some observations record a secret reserve price or an auction price but not both.  These observations, or observations
9:316:29236:15:12:72:526:207:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:with incorrectly recorded bargaining sequences (such as a seller acceptance followed by a buyer counteroffer) are not suitable
9:317:29362:12:13:72:526:195:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:for my final analysis but are still useful in controlling for observable heterogeneity as explained in Section 4.2.  Missing secret
9:318:29493:13:12:72:526:182:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:reserve prices typically occur when the seller chooses not to report a reserve price,  either planning to be present at the
9:319:29616:12:12:72:526:170:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:auction sale to accept or reject the auction price in person or planning to have the auction house call her on the phone
9:320:29736:12:13:72:526:158:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:rather than determining a reserve price ex-ante.  Missing auction prices can occur due to the descending/ascending practice
9:321:29859:13:12:72:526:145:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:of auctioneers:  auctioneers do not start the bidding at zero; they start the bidding high and then lower the price until a
9:322:29982:12:12:72:526:133:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:bidder indicates a willingness to pay, at which point the ascending auction begins.  If bidders are slow to participate, the
9:323:30106:12:12:72:526:121:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:auctioneer will cease to lower bids and postpone the sale of the vehicle until a later date, leaving no auction price recorded.
9:324:30233:12:0:72:249:109:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:See Lacetera, Larsen, Pope, and Sydnor (2014).

11:405:33465:364:15:72:517:293:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:F:Notes:  Fleet/lease sample.  For each period (period 1 = auction and immediately following, period 2 = seller’s
11:406:33576:15:14:72:479:278:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:F:first turn in post-auction bargaining, period 3 = buyer’s first turn, etc.), table reports the number of
11:407:33680:14:14:72:509:264:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:F:observations ending in that period, percent of total sample ending in that period, and percent of time which
11:408:33788:14:14:72:524:250:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:F:trade occurred.  Table also reports reserve price and auction price for observations ending in a given period and,
11:409:33902:14:32:72:505:236:9:g_d0_f11:9:g_d0_f11:F:for those observations ending in trade, the reserve price, auction price, and final price conditional on trade.
11:410:34013:32:16:72:526:204:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Period 1 is the auction.  Observations ending in period 1 represent cases which ended with the auction
11:411:34115:16:14:72:466:188:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:or immediately thereafter, and hence cases for which no bargaining actions are recorded.18
11:412:34205:14:16:79:525:174:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:18When the reserve is not met, the game may still end immediately (and very frequently does–61.26% of the time in the
11:413:34322:16:12:72:526:158:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:dealers sample and 45.65% of the time in the fleet/lease sample (not shown in Tables 2–3)—when one of the following is
11:414:34440:12:12:72:526:146:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:true:  1) the seller is present at the auction house and can immediately accept or reject the auction price; 2) the auctioneer
11:415:34566:12:12:72:526:134:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:rejects the auction price on behalf of the seller,  knowing that alternating-offer bargaining is unlikely to occur;  or 3) the
11:416:34692:12:13:72:526:122:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:high bidder walks away from bargaining before the seller is contacted.  The third case is observable in the data, and occurs
11:417:34816:13:12:72:526:109:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:1.32% of the time in the dealers sample and 0.59% of the time in the fleet/lease sample (not shown in Tables 2–3).  The
11:418:34935:12:0:72:526:97:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:first two cases are indistinguishable in the data, and therefore I assume that any time the auctioneer chooses to reject the
12:419:35061:0:15:86:525:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The  remaining  periods  are  labeled  with  even  numbers  for  seller  turns  and  odd  numbers  for  buyer
12:420:35170:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:turns.  Period 2 is the seller’s first turn in the post-auction bargaining game, and observations ending in
12:421:35277:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:this period represent cases in which the seller accepted the auction price or quit.  Period 3 is the buyer’s
12:422:35385:15:16:72:526:635:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:first turn in bargaining, and is reached only if the seller chose to counter in period 2.  Play continues back
12:423:35495:16:16:72:384:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and forth between the buyer and seller until one party accepts or quits.
12:424:35567:16:15:86:525:603:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 2 demonstrates that in 77% of the dealers sample the game ends at the auction, and in these
12:425:35664:15:16:72:526:588:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:cases the final price when trade happens (which occurs 81% of the time) is naturally the auction price.
12:426:35767:16:15:72:526:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Consider now the fifth period of the game.  Only 1.4% of the full sample reaches the this period,  but
12:427:35869:15:16:72:526:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:this still consists of nearly 2,000 observations.  In the fifth period, when trade does occur, it occurs at
12:428:35976:16:15:72:525:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:an average final price ($7,650) which is over$600 above the average auction price ($7,005), but still does
12:429:36082:15:16:71:525:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:not reach as high as the average reserve price ($8,523).  Overall, Table 2 suggests that observations which
12:430:36189:16:15:71:525:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:ended in later periods had somewhat higher reserve prices than those ending in earlier periods, consistent
12:431:36295:15:16:71:525:495:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with Coasian dynamics (see Fudenberg and Tirole 1991).  Only 3 buyer-seller pairs endured ten periods of
12:432:36399:16:15:71:525:479:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the game, all of them coming to agreement in the end, at an average price over$1,000 above the average
12:433:36501:15:16:71:130:464:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auction price.
12:434:36515:16:15:86:525:448:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 3 displays similar patterns for the fleet/lease sample.  82% of the sample ended the game at the
12:435:36617:15:16:71:525:433:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auction.  Less than one percent ended in the fifth period, but this still consists of over 1,000 observations.
12:436:36727:16:16:71:525:417:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Buyer-seller pairs who traded in this round did so at an average of about$600 above the auction price.
12:437:36829:16:15:71:525:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Other than observations ending in the first period, observations with phone bargaining ending in later
12:438:36931:15:16:71:525:386:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:periods  had  higher  average  reserve  prices  than  those  ending  in  earlier  periods,  again  consistent  with
12:439:37046:16:15:71:525:370:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Coasian dynamics.  Three pairs remained for nine periods of the game, with only one of the three ending
12:440:37149:15:39:71:108:355:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in trade.
12:441:37158:39:25:71:525:316:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:3    Model of Post-Auction Bargaining with Secret Reserve Price
12:442:37221:25:15:71:525:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This section presents a model of the auction-followed-by-bargaining mechanism used at wholesale auto
12:443:37321:15:16:71:525:276:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auctions.  I first discuss the timing of the mechanism and set up some general assumptions.  I discuss each
12:444:37428:16:16:71:525:260:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:stage of the game, starting from the end with the post-auction bargaining stage.  I then present a model
12:445:37532:16:15:71:525:244:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the ascending auction stage, demonstrating that truth-telling is a weakly dominant strategy, and the
12:446:37635:15:16:71:525:229:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:stage in which the seller chooses a secret reserve price, demonstrating that the seller’s strategy is strictly
12:447:37745:16:15:71:117:213:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:increasing.
12:448:37756:15:24:86:366:198:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The timing of the game at wholesale auto auctions is as follows:
12:449:37820:24:23:84:237:174:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:1.  Seller sets a secret reserve price.
12:450:37859:23:24:84:265:151:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:2.  N bidders bid in an ascending auction.
12:451:37901:24:17:84:458:127:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:3.  If the auction price exceeds the secret reserve price, the high bidder wins the item.
12:452:37990:17:0:72:488:110:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:auction price on behalf of the seller this choice coincides with what the seller would have done given the chance.
13:453:38106:0:15:84:525:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:4.  If the auction price does not exceed the secret reserve price, the high bidder is given the opportunity
13:454:38213:15:23:96:351:666:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:A:to walk away, or to enter into bargaining with the seller.19
13:455:38273:23:16:84:525:643:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:5.  If the high bidder chooses to enter bargaining, the auction price becomes the first bargaining offer,
13:456:38378:16:15:96:525:627:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:and the high bidder and seller enter an alternating-offer bargaining game, mediated by the auction
13:457:38476:15:22:96:123:612:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:house.
13:458:38482:22:22:86:305:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Throughout I maintain the following assumptions:
13:459:38530:22:22:72:139:568:10:g_d0_f12:10:g_d0_f12:C:Assumptions.
13:460:38542:22:16:71:526:546:10:g_d0_f13:10:g_d0_f13:C:(A1)  The ascending auction follows a button auction model withN≥2risk-neutral bidders participating.
13:461:38643:16:16:96:525:530:10:g_d0_f13:10:g_d0_f13:C:Fori= 1,...,N, each buyerihas a private valuation ̃Bi=W+Bi, withW∼FW,Bi∼FB, and
13:462:38722:16:22:96:213:514:10:g_d0_f13:10:g_d0_f13:C:withBiindependent ofW.
13:463:38744:22:16:71:525:492:10:g_d0_f13:10:g_d0_f13:C:(A2)  The  risk-neutral  seller  has  a  private  valuation ̃S=W+S,  whereS∼FS,  withSindependent  of
13:464:38845:16:23:96:181:476:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f13:C:WandBifor alli.
13:465:38860:23:15:71:525:453:10:g_d0_f13:10:g_d0_f13:C:(A3)  In bargaining players face a per-offer disutility,(cB,cS)>0, as well as discount factor,δ∈[0,1),
13:466:38962:15:22:96:454:438:10:g_d0_f13:10:g_d0_f13:C:where1−δrepresents the probability that bargaining will break down exogenously.
13:467:39041:22:16:86:525:416:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:I further assumeFB,FS, andFWhave corresponding atomless densitiesfB,fS, andfW, with supports
13:468:39133:16:15:72:487:400:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:[B,B], [S,S], and (−∞,∞).  Let realizations of these random variables be denotedb,s, andw.
13:469:39223:15:16:86:525:385:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The random variableWis observed by all buyers and the seller. In the estimation framework in Section
13:470:39323:16:15:72:525:369:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:4, I allowWto be unobservable to the econometrician but observed by all players, thus allowing for all
13:471:39425:15:16:72:525:354:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:players’ values to be correlated.20Conditional onW, buyers and sellers have independent private values
13:472:39527:16:15:72:526:338:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(IPV). A motivation for this framework is that buyers—as well as dealer-type sellers—have valuations
13:473:39627:15:16:72:525:323:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:arising primarily from their local demand and inventory needs.21Also, seller valuations can depend on
13:474:39728:16:16:72:526:307:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the value at which the car was assessed as a trade-in, or, for a bank or leasing company, valuations can
13:475:39832:16:14:72:526:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:arise  from  the  size  of  the  defaulted  loan.22The  button  auction  model  is  a  natural  choice  given  that
13:476:39947:14:15:79:525:277:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:19At wholesale auto auctions, some large sellers are given the option to elect to eliminate step 4 above, implying that
13:477:40066:15:12:72:526:262:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:when the auction price does not meet the secret reserve price, the high bidder is not allowed to immediately walk away
13:478:40184:12:12:72:526:250:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:from bargaining but must wait until the seller responds to the auction price.  This situation is referred to as a “binding-if
13:479:40309:12:13:72:526:238:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:auction.”  It can be shown that in a binding-if auction, the seller’s secret reserve price strategy is only guaranteed to be
13:480:40433:13:12:72:526:225:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:weakly increasing, rather than strictly as in the non-binding-if case.  It can also be shown that bidders will not necessarily
13:481:40559:12:12:72:526:213:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:drop out of bidding at their valuations but may instead drop out at a price slightly below their valuation to account for
13:482:40680:12:13:72:526:201:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:the possibility of paying bargaining costs.  In the data, there is no way to know if a sale took place in a binding-if setting.
13:483:40807:13:7:72:242:188:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:I treat all auctions as non-binding-if auctions.
13:484:40855:7:10:79:525:181:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:20I also introduce characteristics observable toboththe econometrician and all players and I control for these observables.
13:485:40978:10:16:79:525:171:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:21Conversations with buyers, sellers, and auction house employees support this assumption:  buyers claim to decide upon
13:486:41097:16:12:72:526:155:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:their willingness to pay before bidding begins, often having a specific retail customer lined up for a particular car.  See Lang
13:487:41225:12:12:72:526:143:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:(2011).  Studying Korean auto auctions, Kim and Lee (2014) tested and failed to reject the IPV assumption, while Roberts
13:488:41345:12:8:72:379:131:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:(2013) found evidence of unobserved auction-level heterogeneity (analogous toW).
13:489:41425:8:15:79:525:123:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:22These  explanations  for  seller  values  are  due  to  conversations  with  industry  professionals.   Note  also  that  adverse
13:490:41556:15:12:72:526:108:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:selection  from  the  seller  possessing  more  knowledge  about  car  quality  than  the  buyer  is  likely  small  because  of  auction
13:491:41693:12:0:72:479:96:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:house information revelation requirements and because sellers are not previous owners/drivers of the vehicles.
14:492:41805:0:15:72:525:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:jump bidding is rare—as it is the auctioneer who calls out bids—and bid increments are small.23The
14:493:41903:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:assumption  of  symmetry  is  not  strong  in  this  setting  given  that  buyer  identities  are  unknown  to  the
14:494:42018:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:seller in bargaining and given the assumption of a private values button auction, implying that bidders’
14:495:42122:15:16:72:525:635:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auction strategies will not depend on the identities of other participants.24The parameterδcaptures the
14:496:42225:16:16:72:526:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:feature that bargaining may end through an auction house employee failing to follow up on a bargaining
14:497:42327:16:15:72:320:603:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:sequence, occurring in 1–2% of bargaining interactions.25
14:498:42384:15:16:86:525:588:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In what follows, I demonstrate three properties which prove useful for estimation: 1) a buyer’s auction
14:499:42487:16:15:72:526:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:strategy is to drop out at his value, as in a standard ascending auction, 2) the seller’s secret reserve price
14:500:42597:15:16:72:525:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:strategy,ρ(S), is strictly increasing in her typeS, and 3) auction-level heterogeneity shifts the outcomes
14:501:42703:16:15:72:525:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the game in a tractable manner.  I begin by modeling the game conditional on a realization ofWand
14:502:42803:15:16:72:525:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:thus omitWfor notational simplicity and return to it in Section 3.4.  I ignore auction house fees in this
14:503:42908:16:32:72:474:510:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:theoretical analysis but account for their existence in the empirical analysis in Section 4.1.26
14:504:43004:32:22:72:203:478:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:C:3.1    Bargaining Stage
14:505:43027:22:15:72:526:456:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This section describes a simple model of the dynamic, post-auction bargaining game.  The game begins
14:506:43127:15:16:72:525:441:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with an offer by the buyer in periodt= 1. At wholesale auto auctions, this offer is the price at the auction.
14:507:43236:16:15:72:526:425:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The seller then chooses between accepting (A), quitting (Q)—meaning terminating the negotiations—
14:508:43333:15:16:72:526:410:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:or making a counteroffer (C). Accepting ends the game, with trade taking place at the accepted price.
14:509:43434:16:16:72:526:394:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Quitting also ends the game, with no trade taking place.  After a counteroffer by the seller, play returns
14:510:43540:16:15:72:525:378:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to the buyer, who then chooses between accepting, quitting, and countering.  Thus, atteven it is the
14:511:43640:15:16:72:525:363:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:seller’s turn, and attodd it is the buyer’s turn.  Below, I refer to period “t” as being the seller’s turn and
14:512:43750:16:15:72:525:347:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:period “t+ 1” as being the buyer’s turn.  Where useful for clarification, I also include the superscripts
14:513:43855:15:16:72:441:332:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:“S” or “B” in notation to denote an action taken by the seller or buyer respectively.
14:514:43940:16:15:86:525:316:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:LetHt≡ {Pτ}t−1τ=1represent  the  set  of  offers  made  from  period  1  up  through  periodt−1.   The
14:515:44042:15:16:72:525:301:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:player whose turn it is at timethas not yet made an offer and so this offer does not enter intoHt.  Let
14:516:44145:16:15:71:525:285:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f3:B:DSt∈{A,Q,C}represent the seller’s decision in periodt, and letDBt+1∈{A,Q,C}represent the buyer’s
14:517:44241:15:16:72:176:270:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:decision in periodt+ 1.
14:518:44264:16:15:86:525:254:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The seller’s payoff at timetis given by the following.  Conditional on the history of offersHt, which
14:519:44365:15:17:72:525:239:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:includes the buyer’s most recent offerPBt−1, a seller of typeS=s, chooses to accept (A), quit (Q), or
14:520:44466:17:10:79:240:222:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:23Bid increments lie between$25 and$100.
14:521:44506:10:10:79:525:212:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:24See Coey, Larsen, and Sweeney (2014) for a formal treatment of the implications of asymmetries in ascending auctions.
14:522:44625:10:15:79:525:202:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:25This number is based on the percent of bargaining sequence records in which trade failed and the sequence of offers was

16:614:47703:0:22:72:251:681:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:C:3.2    Ascending Auction Stage

16:638:48533:16:16:72:526:425:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:but with the auction price as the buyer’s counteroffer, rather than the maximizing counteroffer.  In this
16:639:48638:16:23:72:227:409:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:setup, the following property holds:
16:640:48674:23:16:72:526:386:10:g_d0_f12:10:g_d0_f13:C:Proposition 1.If in the bargaining game the seller never accepts offers below the auction price, truth-
16:641:48777:16:23:72:418:370:10:g_d0_f13:10:g_d0_f13:C:telling is weakly dominant for bidders in the auction stage.  That is,βi(bi) =bi.
16:642:48858:23:31:86:244:347:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:All proofs are found in Appendix A.
16:643:48893:31:16:86:525:316:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:This result implies that the winning bid will be the second order statistic from the distribution of
16:644:48993:16:15:71:525:300:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:buyer valuations, as in standard ascending or second price auctions.29Intuitively, the assumption that
16:645:49095:15:16:71:525:285:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the seller never accepts bargaining offers below the auction price ensures that buyers will not be tempted
16:646:49201:16:15:71:525:269:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to bid beyond their valuations in the ascending auction stage in hopes of bargaining to a lower price in the
16:647:49309:15:16:71:525:254:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:post-auction bargaining stage.  This is supported by the data:  the bargained price is not lower than the
16:648:49414:16:15:71:525:238:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:auction price (see Tables 2–3).  Moreover, bidders are not tempted to drop out before the bidding reaches
16:649:49519:15:16:71:525:223:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:their valuations because if the high bidder learns that the auction price did not meet the secret reserve,
16:650:49625:16:16:71:525:207:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:he can always opt out of bargaining, and I assume this opting out is costless.  And because the auction
16:651:49728:16:15:71:525:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:price is the second-highest valuation of the bidders, the seller cannot infer anything about the valuation
16:652:49834:15:16:71:525:176:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the winner other than learning the point at which the buyer distribution is truncated, eliminating any
16:653:49939:16:15:71:525:160:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:incentive of buyers to shade bids downward to avoid revealing information to the seller.  The argument
16:654:50041:15:15:71:506:145:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for identifying the distribution of buyer valuations, discussed in Section 4.4, relies on Proposition 1.
16:655:50145:15:15:79:525:130:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:29Huh and Park (2010) found the same result in a theoretical model of second price auctions with complete informa-
16:656:50259:15:12:72:526:115:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:tion (rather than incomplete information) post-auction bargaining:  bidders’ strategies were unaffected by the presence of
16:657:50381:12:0:72:113:103:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:bargaining.

17:699:51490:24:23:72:467:416:10:g_d0_f12:10:g_d0_f13:C:Proposition 2.The seller’s optimal secret reserve price,ρ∗(s), is strictly increasing ins.
17:700:51580:23:16:86:525:393:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The intuition behind Proposition 2 is that the secret reserve price is never revealed and hence the

17:703:51906:16:15:72:526:346:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:on  bargaining  being  costly  to  buyers  (Assumption  A3),  such  that  some  buyers  will  choose  to  opt  out

17:706:52205:15:16:86:525:300:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The approach presented in Section 4.5 for identifying the distribution of seller valuations does not

23:947:67198:15:16:72:526:584:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:weakly increasing, following the rearrangement approach of Chernozhukov, Fernandez-Val, and Galichon
23:948:67298:16:16:72:525:568:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(2009).41Let ̃L∗and ̃U∗represent the bounds onF ̃Safter enforcing (8) and after enforcing monotonicity.
23:949:67401:16:15:72:525:552:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Finally, these estimates are not necessarily surjective/onto;  that is, there may not exist values ofVat
23:950:67505:15:16:72:526:537:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:which sellers accept or quit with probability approaching 0 or 1.  I fill in these missing parts of the upper
23:951:67614:16:15:72:525:521:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and lower bounds by assuming a constant gap between quantiles ofF ̃Rand those of the upper or lower
23:952:67713:15:16:72:143:506:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:bounds onF ̃S.42
23:953:67729:16:15:86:525:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:After  estimatingFUSandFLS,  the  final  upper  and  lower  bounds onFS,  I obtain  draws  from  these
23:954:67831:15:16:72:526:475:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:CDFs by inverting them at random uniform draws.  I estimate the corresponding densities using kernel
23:955:67931:16:32:72:228:459:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f4:B:density estimation of these draws.43
23:956:67967:32:22:72:237:427:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:C:4.6    Distribution Estimates
23:957:67996:22:15:72:526:405:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This section presents the results of the estimation procedure described in Sections 4.1–4.5.  After control-
23:958:68104:15:16:72:526:390:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:ling for auction house fees and observable auction-level heterogeneity as described in Sections 4.1–4.2, I
23:959:68210:16:15:72:526:374:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:perform the likelihood approach described in Section 4.3 to account for unobserved auction-level hetero-
23:960:68314:15:16:72:526:359:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:geneity.  Figure 1 displays the densities of reserve prices and auction prices before removing unobserved
23:961:68419:16:16:72:525:343:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:heterogeneity (fR+WandfB(2)+W) and after removing unobserved heterogeneity (fRandfB(2)), as well
23:962:68515:16:15:72:525:327:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:as the estimated density of observed heterogeneity (fW).  In each panel of Figure 1, and in the tables
23:963:68617:15:15:72:525:312:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and figures which follow, monetary values are denoted in units of$1,000 and should be interpreted as
23:964:68717:15:15:79:525:297:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:41In this setting,  rearrangement is performed by simply sorting the vector of lower bounds (estimated on a uniformly
23:965:68834:15:12:72:526:282:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:spaced grid) and reassigning them to the original grid points, and similarly for the upper bounds.  Chernozhukov, Fernandez-
23:966:68958:12:13:72:526:270:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Val,  and Galichon (2009) demonstrated that,  when estimating a monotone function,  a rearranged estimate is always an
23:967:69076:13:12:72:526:257:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:improvement, in terms of estimation error, over an original, non-monotonic estimate.  In practice, the approach is sensitive
23:968:69200:12:12:72:525:245:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:to extreme outliers, and so for this estimation I do not include the top and bottom 0.1% of observations ofV, where the
23:969:69319:12:12:72:526:233:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:kernel regression estimates are noisy.  In practice, the rearrangement has only little impact on the estimates, smoothing out
23:970:69444:12:9:72:238:221:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:small non-monotonic portions of the bounds.
23:971:69487:9:15:79:524:212:6:g_d0_f5:6:g_d0_f20:F:42Specifically, to fill in values of ̃L∗(·) and ̃U∗(·) so that these functions are onto, I do the following.  Let{vm}m=1,...,M
23:972:69613:15:10:72:525:197:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:represent the grid of points on which ̃L∗(·) is evaluated, which can lie beyond the range ofVin the data, in which case
23:973:69732:10:34:72:487:187:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F: ̃L∗(v) returns the empty set.  LetvL= arg minm: ̃L∗(vm)6=∅ ̃L∗(vm) andvL= arg maxm: ̃L∗(vm)6=∅ ̃L∗(vm).  Then
23:974:69842:34:0:209:241:153:8:g_d0_f8:8:g_d0_f23:F:FL ̃S(v)≡
23:975:69851:0:7:243:251:172:8:g_d0_f25:8:g_d0_f25:F:
23:976:69852:7:17:243:251:165:8:g_d0_f25:8:g_d0_f25:F:
23:977:69854:17:0:243:251:148:8:g_d0_f25:8:g_d0_f25:F:
23:978:69856:0:10:256:364:165:8:g_d0_f8:6:g_d0_f20:F:F ̃R(v+vL−v1)ifv < vL
23:979:69877:10:15:257:381:155:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F: ̃L∗(v)ifv∈[vL,vL]
23:980:69895:15:17:256:364:140:8:g_d0_f8:6:g_d0_f20:F:F ̃R(v+vL−vM)ifv >vL
23:981:69915:17:10:72:525:123:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F:Finally, to remove unobserved heterogeneity,FLSis given byFLS(v)≡∫FL ̃S(v−w)fW(w)dw.  I follow the same steps for
23:982:70028:10:10:73:94:113:8:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f6:F: ̃U∗(·).
23:983:70036:10:0:79:378:103:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:43As above, I use a Gaussian kernel with the asymptotically optimal bandwidth.
24:984:70116:0:15:72:525:681:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:follows:  for a car with vector of observablesx, the overall reserve price—less the auction house fee—will
24:985:70222:15:16:72:525:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:have densityfR+W, shifted by the quantityx′γ.  Similarly, for a car with observablesxand unobserved
24:986:70321:16:15:72:526:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:heterogeneityw, the overall density will befR, shifted by the quantityx′γ+w.  A similar interpretation
24:987:70423:15:16:72:183:635:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:follows for auction prices.
24:988:70450:16:16:86:525:619:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Figure 1 shows that the density after removing unobserved heterogeneity,fR(in red), is tighter than
24:989:70549:16:15:72:525:603:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f3:B:fR+W(in  blue)  in  both  the  dealers  sample  (a)  and  the  fleet/lease  sample  (b),  although  more  so  for
24:990:70662:15:16:72:526:588:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:panel (a), suggesting that unobserved heterogeneity plays a bigger role in explaining price variation in
24:991:70766:16:15:72:526:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:reserve prices in dealer cars than in fleet/lease cars.  Panel (a) suggests that,  after accounting for fees
24:992:70874:15:16:72:526:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and observed and unobserved heterogeneity, the residual of sellers’ reserve prices varies principally over a
24:993:70982:16:15:72:525:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:range of about$4,000, ranging from about$0 to$4,000.  In this paper, this residual variation is attributed
24:994:71088:15:16:72:323:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to variation in the seller’s private valuation for the car,s.
24:995:71149:16:15:86:525:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Panels (c) and (d) demonstrate that the results are similar when comparing the density of the auction
24:996:71250:15:16:72:525:495:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:price with unobserved heterogeneity,fB(2)+W,  to the density without unobserved heterogeneity,fB(2),
24:997:71350:16:15:72:525:479:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in  the  dealers  (c)  and  fleet/lease  samples  (d).   In  each  panel  the  densityfB(2)is  centered  about  zero
24:998:71466:15:16:72:525:464:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:because the estimation procedure of 4.3 constrainsE[B(2)] = 0 as a normalization required to apply the
24:999:71568:16:15:72:282:448:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:identification argument due to Kotlarski (1967).
24:1000:71616:15:16:86:525:433:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Figure  2  displays  the  estimated  density  of  unobserved  heterogeneity,fW,  in  the  dealers  (a)  and
24:1001:71723:16:16:72:525:417:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:fleet/lease  samples  (b).   The  majority  of  the  mass  of  unobserved  heterogeneity  lies  between  [-$2,500,
24:1002:71837:16:15:72:104:401:10:g_d0_f385R:10:g_d0_f3:B:$2,500].
24:1003:71845:15:16:86:525:386:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:As a measure of the goodness of fit of this deconvolution procedure, I compare the correlation of the
24:1004:71946:16:15:72:526:370:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:reserve price and auction price (after adjusting for auction house fees) in the raw data to the correlation
24:1005:72053:15:16:72:525:355:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:between simulated draws for ̃Rand ̃B(2), generated by 10,000 draws from the estimated distributions for
24:1006:72156:16:15:72:525:339:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f3:B:FR,FB(2), andFW.  The correlations agree quite closely:  In the dealers sample, the raw vs.  simulated
24:1007:72258:15:16:72:526:324:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:correlation is 0.6146 vs.  0.6149, and in the fleet/lease sample the two quantities are 0.3817 vs.  0.3705.
24:1008:72365:16:15:72:526:308:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:As a further check of fit, note that the model assumes that auction-level heterogeneity—both observed
24:1009:72466:15:16:72:526:293:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and unobserved—is additively separable.  Therefore, the difference between the reserve and auction price
24:1010:72570:16:15:72:525:277:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in the raw data, after removing auction house fees but with heterogeneity included (Rh−B(2),h), should
24:1011:72672:15:16:72:526:262:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:be  equal  in  distribution  to  the  simulated  difference  between  the  reserve  price  and  auction  price  after
24:1012:72789:16:16:72:525:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:removing heterogeneity (R−B(2)).  Figure 3 displays the raw and simulated densities of this difference,
24:1013:72892:16:15:72:219:230:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:which agree well in both samples.
24:1014:72925:15:16:86:525:215:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Before discussing the estimated densities of buyer valuations (from the order statistic approach de-

24:1016:73140:15:16:72:526:184:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:distribution of seller valuations as described in Section 4.5.  Panels (a) and (b) display, for each value of
24:1017:73249:16:15:72:525:168:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the auction price, the probability a seller accepts (Pr(dS2=A), the lower bound, in red) and the probabil-

28:1188:76792:15:16:72:526:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:price (including observed heterogeneity), which should stochastically dominate the distribution of seller
28:1189:76897:16:15:72:525:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:valuations with unobserved heterogeneity included,F ̃S.  To enforce this, I replace any values of the upper

28:1192:77210:16:15:72:525:603:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:which are surjective (covering the range of probabilities in [0,1]) by imposing a fixed distance between

28:1194:77416:16:15:86:525:572:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Figure 4 suggests that, for dealer cars, panels (a) and (c), when the auction price—less the auction
28:1195:77516:15:16:72:525:557:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:house  fee—is  about$2,500  lower  than  expected  (based  on  observable  heterogeneity),  sellers  choose  to
28:1196:77627:16:15:72:526:541:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:accept this auction price with probability about 0.5 and choose to not walk away/quit with probability
28:1197:77729:15:16:72:525:526:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:about 0.75.  Therefore, the probability that ̃Sis less than -$2,500 lies in [0.5,0.75].  The bounds are similar
28:1198:77840:16:15:72:526:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for fleet/lease cars, panels (b) and (d), although these bounds appear to be somewhat tighter and the
28:1199:77941:15:16:72:350:495:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:upper and lower bounds individually appear to be less disperse.
28:1200:78004:16:15:86:525:479:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Figure  5  displays  the  estimated  densities  corresponding  to  the  upper  and  lower  bounds  of  seller
28:1201:78113:15:16:72:525:464:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:valuations after integrating out the unobserved heterogeneity (W).  Panels (a) and (b) display the density
28:1202:78219:16:15:72:526:448:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:corresponding to the upper bound in the dealers and fleet/lease samples, and panels (c) and (d) display
28:1203:78322:15:16:72:526:433:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:similar results for the lower bound.  As with the densities for reserve prices and auction prices in Figure
28:1204:78429:16:16:72:440:417:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:1, the density of seller valuations is tighter after removing unobserved heterogeneity.
28:1205:78516:16:15:86:526:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The estimates of the density of buyer valuations,fB, from the order statistic inversion described in
28:1206:78616:15:16:72:525:386:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Section  4.4  are  shown  in  Figure  6  in  green.   The  density  of  the  auction  price,fB(2),  in  Figure  6  (in
28:1207:78734:16:15:72:526:370:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:blue) is equivalent to the density shown in panels (c) and (d) of Figure 1,  after removing unobserved
28:1208:78836:15:16:72:525:355:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:heterogeneity.  As should be the case,fB(2)first-order stochastically dominatesfB.  Figure 6 also displays
28:1209:78942:16:15:72:498:339:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(in red) the density corresponding to the upper or lower bound of seller valuations from Figure 5.
28:1210:79040:15:16:86:525:324:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Figure 6 demonstrates that there is significant overlap, both in terms of support and mass, between
28:1211:79139:16:15:72:526:308:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the density of buyer valuations and seller valuations.  This feature of overlapping supports is referred to in
28:1212:79249:15:16:72:526:293:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the theoretical bargaining literature as the “no gap” case (i.e., there is no gap between the upper bound
28:1213:79354:16:15:72:526:277:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the support of seller valuations and the lower bound of the support of buyer valuations,  and hence
28:1214:79456:15:16:72:526:262:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:there is uncertainty as to whether gains from trade actually exist), and is the case motivating Myerson
28:1215:79559:16:16:72:525:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and Satterthwaite (1983).44Comparing panel (a) to panel (b) shows that fleet/lease sellers tend to have
28:1216:79662:16:15:72:526:230:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:more mass in the lower half of the support of seller types than do dealers, suggesting that trade may be
28:1217:79766:15:38:72:437:215:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:more likely to occur in the fleet/lease sample, as is shown to be the case in Table 1.
28:1218:79852:38:26:72:437:177:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:5    The Pareto Frontier and Real-World Bargaining
28:1219:79902:26:15:72:526:151:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This section describes how the Pareto frontier and other counterfactual mechanisms can be solved for once
28:1220:80007:15:16:72:526:136:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the distributions of seller and buyer valuations are known.  I also describe identification and estimation
28:1221:80113:16:15:72:526:120:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the direct mechanism corresponding to the real-world, dynamic bargaining mechanism used at auto
28:1222:80211:15:0:79:211:105:6:g_d0_f5:8:g_d0_f6:F:44See Fudenberg and Tirole (1991).
